Three protesters were arrested at the scene.
Cyrus Reynolds, 33, of Newmarket faces a single charge of mischief.
Thirty-six year old Hesham Aly of Toronto, and 26-year-old Ali Nasser of Mississauga both face obstruct peace officer charges.
All are scheduled to next appear in court in February.
While protesters and their advocates insist the demonstrations are meant to inform rather than intimidate, the Avenue Road bridge is located in Canada's largest and most diverse Jewish communities, within a kilometre of nine synagogues and several Jewish businesses, homes, schools, daycares and community centres.
Protesters on the bridge were seen carrying signs depicting Adolf Hitler and related Nazi imagery.
Video of Toronto cops delivering coffee to anti-Israel protesters sparks outrage
Toronto police to bar anti-Israel protests from Avenue Road bridge
Jewish residents have said they felt intimidated and harassed by the protests. The demonstrations made international headlines after a video post on social media showed Toronto police officers delivering coffee to protesters,
Chief Demkiw said last Thursday that the protests had escalated to the point where they posed a serious public safety hazard.
"Hate and intimidation can have no space in our city and we have been unwavering in our commitment to restore the sense of public safety, and not tolerate behaviour that crosses the line to criminal activity," Demkiw said.
Pro-Palestinian protests in Toronto began almost immediately after the Oct. 7 attacks, where Hamas terrorists kidnapped hundreds of people and brought them back in Gaza, and went on a wild spree of rape, torture and killing, murdering over 1,000 people, mostly civilians.
Anti-Israel activists soon began picketing outside of Jewish-owned businesses and community centres, and targeting politicians they perceive as being pro-Israel.
Happening now: Montrealers are rallying outside Foreign Affairs Minister Melanie Joly's home, calling for an end to Canada's military and diplomatic support for Israeli genocide.
- Independent Jewish Voices (@IndJewishVoices) January 13, 2024
Protesters set up a noisy demonstration outside of the Montreal home of Foreign Affairs Minister Mélanie Joly on Saturday morning, a day after she issued a government statement that rejected the claims by South Africa at a UN court accusing Israel of "genocide."
About three-dozen protestors appeared at her home, setting up a tent as well as a loud speaker, and accused her of complicity with the alleged "genocide."
Numerous people from across the political spectrum denounced the tactic on X (formerly Twitter).
"This is intimidation not protest. Just like protesting in a predominantly Jewish neighbourhood in Toronto was. Find an office, a consulate, an embassy, a government building, a public square to protest all you want for what you believe in - not a private home," tweeted Conservative deputy leader Melissa Lantsman in response to the crowd gathering at Joly's home.
Anti-Israel demonstrators are expected to disrupt a number of events on Sunday.
A Toronto anti-Israel group plans to sabotage a Sunday afternoon family ice skate at Mel Lastman Square, hosted by Toronto City Councillor Lily Cheng, Liberal MP Ali Ehsassi and Ontario PC MPP Stan Cho.
Similar actions last weekend at a downtown Toronto skate event hosted by Toronto Mayor Olivia Chow saw flag-draped demonstrators standing in the middle of the rink, waving flags and intimidating families and couples trying to enjoy an afternoon on the ice.
Anti-Israel protesters are also expected to disrupt a vigil marking the 100th day of Israeli hostages being held captive by Hamas, scheduled to begin at 3 p.m. at Nathan Phillips Square in downtown Toronto.
Two anti-Israel groups are also planning a 4 p.m. rally at the Israeli consulate at Bloor St. and Yonge.
In addition, starting at 7:30 p.m., a rally of community leaders and politicians in support of Israel will take place at Toronto's Congregation Habonim to mark the hostages' 100 days of captivity, near Bathurst St. and Glencairn Ave.

Three anti-Israel protesters arrested for defying bridge demo ban

Author: Passifiume, Bryan

Publication info: Passifiume, Bryan. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/blogs-podcasts-websites/three-anti-israel-protesters-arrested-defying/docview/2914809808/se-2?accountid=15090

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Apq1busgeneral&amp;atitle=Three+anti-Israel+protesters+arrested+for+defying+bridge+demo+ban&amp;title=National+Post+%28Online%29&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-14&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=Passifiume%2C+Bryan&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=National+Post+%28Online%29&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Anti-Israel protesters once again attempted to occupy the Avenue Road overpass in Toronto, leading to three arrests after the city's police chief declared the overpass off-limits to demonstrations late last week
After video of Toronto police officers delivering coffee to protesters made headlines around the world, Toronto Police Chief Myron Demkiw announced last week that demonstrations would no longer be permitted on the bridge, and that future protestors would face arrest.
Despite that warning, protesters began arriving at the overpass - located amidst Toronto's largest Jewish neighbourhoods - Saturday morning, where police were waiting.
Video posted on social media by Toronto lawyer and commentator Caryma Sa'd depicted Toronto police members squaring-off against anti-Israel protesters, many arguing with police over the chief's order.
Chaotic scene at Avenue Road overpass as police make arrests.
It is unclear how protesters ended up on the bridge to begin with- there was a heavy police presence in the area.
part 1 of 4#cdnpoli #Toronto #Palestine #Israel #Gaza #ProtestMania

Document 26 of 104

Barbara Kay: Defamation case against antisemitic slander only the beginning

Author: Kay, Barbara

Publication info: Kay, Barbara. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/blogs-podcasts-websites/barbara-kay-defamation-case-against-antisemitic/docview/2914567887/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Apq1busgeneral&amp;atitle=Barbara+Kay%3A+Defamation+case+against+antisemitic+slander+only+the+beginning&amp;title=National+Post+%28Online%29&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-14&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=Kay%2C+Barbara&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=National+Post+%28Online%29&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: On July 10, 2020, a statement of claim for defamation was issued against Kimberley Hawkins, owner and "directing mind" of now-closed Toronto Bloor St. eatery, Foodbenders. The plaintiff, Shai DeLuca, a Canadian with Israeli citizenship, is a designer and longtime contributor to CTV's lifestyle show, Cityline. He's also a gay Zionist who served with the IDF as a combat engineer.
Hawkins is an ardently progressive, anti-Zionist activist. She mounted a gigantic "I (heart) Gaza" sign in Foodbenders' window; her social media account warned "Zionists" (taken by many to mean Jews) away from her business. In short, you couldn't ask for any antagonists more passionately divided in their beliefs about Israel or more committed to defending their corner to the bitter end.
The suit was triggered by offensive statements featuring DeLuca's name on Foodbenders' official Instagram account; a gravely problematic one read: "(DeLuca) is an IDF SOLDIER (aka terrorist) yet he's using the BLM movement for likes. How can you sit here and post about BLM when you have your sniper rifle aimed at Palestinian children."
DeLuca was represented by David Elmaleh and David Rosenberg of Re-LAW LLP. They partnered withThe Lawfare Project, which "provides pro bono legal services to protect the civil and human rights of the Jewish people worldwide." Hawkins' lawyer was pro-Palestinian activist Stephen Ellis, who also represents controversial Ontario MPP Sara Jama, who was kicked out of the provincial NDP caucus for antisemitic comments on social media.
On Dec 22, a Superior Court judgment was released, finding DeLuca had been defamed in a series of "abhorrent" antisemitic Instagram posts by Hawkins, and was entitled to $85,000 in damages, which included $10,000 in punitive damages to indicate the court's "outrage" over Hawkins' conduct. The judge found Hawkins "acted with malice," was "irresponsible" and had hoped to end DeLuca's career at Cityline. (Cityline stood by DeLuca. Ironically, Covid and public opprobrium shut Foodbenders down.)
Justice Gina Papageorgiou castigated Hawkins for never apologizing during three years of the suit's progress. "Despite the many cases referred to me involving allegations like the ones here, the Defendants in this case were not deterred," the judge wrote. "These kinds of statements not only affect people's reputations, but they also contribute to prejudice, antisemitism and intolerance and have the potential to incite violence."
Apart from responding to a Canadian Jewish News reporter's query for comment on the judgment with personal insults" directed to their reporter - "bitch," "trash" and "squatter," and "sending a photo of an Israeli soldier who had recently been killed in the fighting in Gaza" - Hawkins has made no public statement. Stephen Ellis who, in an article he wrote midway through the suit, "When the Israel Lobby Comes for You," described certain bona fide Jewish community institutions as, in aggregate, "mendacious, vindictive, and violent agents of the Israeli state," did not respond to a CJN request for comment.
DeLuca's legal team was happy to comment. David Elmaleh observed that the case is the first he is aware of where an IDF veteran in Canada has successfully pursued a defamation lawsuit related to his military service. The result, he feels may well "deter some (but sadly not all) of the antisemitic vitriol that we have seen online targeting Jews, Israelis and the brave IDF soldiers and first responders." Human rights lawyer Brooke Goldstein, the Lawfare Project's Canadian founder and director, commented, "The victory underscores that there will be legal repercussions for those who defame Jewish people under the guise of 'Palestinian advocacy'."
Justice Papageorgiou cautioned against reading larger messages into DeLuca's win, noting in her judgment that defamation suits are meant to "vindicate reputation," not as a "tool to fight racism, antisemitism or other kinds of prejudice." And DeLuca was indeed made whole. But how, in the present circumstances, could this case not punch above its weight? If antisemitism was a serious problem before Oct. 7 - and it was - it was a mostly submerged iceberg in terms of public perception. Now the massive iceberg is on full display in all its staggering weight.
Nevertheless, defamation suits on this file cannot treat antisemitism's root causes; they can only treat its symptoms. As one huge root cause of flagrant antisemitism, the universities have long cried out for a reckoning. Fortunately, the current ferment in the U.S. over Harvard University and other Ivy League antisemitism enablers is also bubbling up here.
The Lawfare Project recently partnered with the law firm of Diamond and Diamond personal injuries Lawyers to file a $77 million class action suit against McMaster University and the McMaster Students' Union. A Jan. 3 statement of claim alleges that "McMaster University has become, for over a decade, home to systemic discrimination, harassment, hatred, antisemitism, violent discourse, and actual or threatened physical harm amongst the student body." Plaintiffs are all currently enrolled students and those who graduated within the last two years.
In fact, Diamond and Diamond already have five other prominent universities (plus many of their student unions) - Queen's, York, Concordia, Toronto Metropolitain and the University of British Columbia - in their proposed crosshairs on the same grounds. Diamonds' managing partner Sandra Zisckind told CTV news, "You should not be allowed to bring the Hamas flag to a university." As for free speech rights, "You can disagree with Israel, you can say there needs to be a ceasefire, those are things that are tantamount to free speech, but you cannot say we need to kill the Jews."
If the suits go forward - none of the claims have been certified by the courts - a win would be tantamount to an immediately brutal, but ultimately healing form of chemotherapy on a long-metastizing social cancer. A loss? Let's not even go there.
kaybarb@gmail.com
X: @BarbaraRKay

Document 32 of 104

Defamation case only the beginning; 'Palestinian advocacy' no excuse for antisemitism

Author: Kay, Barbara

Publication info: Kay, Barbara. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/defamation-case-only-beginning-palestinian/docview/2913780513/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Apq1busgeneral&amp;atitle=Defamation+case+only+the+beginning%3B+%27Palestinian+advocacy%27+no+excuse+for+antisemitism&amp;title=National+Post&amp;issn=14868008&amp;date=2024-01-13&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.17&amp;au=Kay%2C+Barbara&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=National+Post&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: On July 10, 2020, a statement of claim for defamation was issued against Kimberley Hawkins, owner and "directing mind" of now-closed Toronto Bloor St. eatery, Foodbenders. The plaintiff, Shai DeLuca, a Canadian with Israeli citizenship, is a designer and longtime contributor to CTV's lifestyle show, Cityline. He's also a gay Zionist who served with the IDF as a combat engineer.
Hawkins is an ardently progressive, anti-Zionist activist. She mounted a gigantic "I (heart) Gaza" sign in Foodbenders'window; her social media account warned "Zionists" (taken by many to mean Jews) away from her business. In short, you couldn't ask for any antagonists more passionately divided in their beliefs about Israel or more committed to defending their corner to the bitter end.
The suit was triggered by offensive statements featuring DeLuca's name on Foodbenders'official Instagram account; a gravely problematic one read: "(DeLuca) is an IDF SOLDIER (a.k.a. terrorist) yet he's using the BLM movement for likes. How can you sit here and post about BLM when you have your sniper rifle aimed at Palestinian children."
DeLuca was represented by David Elmaleh and David Rosenberg of Re-LAW LLP. They partnered with The Lawfare Project, which "provides pro bono legal services to protect the civil and human rights of the Jewish people worldwide." Hawkins's lawyer was pro-Palestinian activist Stephen Ellis, who also represents controversial Ontario MPP Sara Jama, who was kicked out of the provincial NDP caucus for antisemitic comments on social media.
On Dec 22, a Superior Court judgment was released, finding DeLuca had been defamed in a series of "abhorrent" antisemitic Instagram posts by Hawkins, and was entitled to $85,000 in damages, which included $10,000 in punitive damages to indicate the court's "outrage" over Hawkins's conduct. The judge found Hawkins "acted with malice," was "irresponsible" and had hoped to end DeLuca's career at Cityline. (Cityline stood by DeLuca. Ironically, COVID and public opprobrium shut Foodbenders down.)
Justice Gina Papageorgiou castigated Hawkins for never apologizing during three years of the suit's progress. "Despite the many cases referred to me involving allegations like the ones here, the Defendants in this case were not deterred," the judge wrote. "These kinds of statements not only affect people's reputations, but they also contribute to prejudice, antisemitism and intolerance and have the potential to incite violence."
Apart from responding to a Canadian Jewish News query for comment on the judgment with personal insults directed to their reporter - "bitch," "trash" and "squatter," and "sending a photo of an Israeli soldier who had recently been killed in the fighting in Gaza" - Hawkins has made no public statement.
Stephen Ellis, who in an article he wrote midway through the suit "When the Israel Lobby Comes for You," described certain bona fide Jewish community institutions as, in aggregate, "mendacious, vindictive, and violent agents of the Israeli state," did not respond to a CJN request for comment.
DeLuca's legal team was happy to comment. David Elmaleh observed that the case is the first he is aware of where an IDF veteran in Canada has successfully pursued a defamation lawsuit related to his military service. The result, he feels, may well "deter some (but sadly not all) of the antisemitic vitriol that we have seen online targeting Jews, Israelis and the brave IDF soldiers and first responders." Human rights lawyer Brooke Goldstein, the Lawfare Project's Canadian founder and director, commented, "The victory underscores that there will be legal repercussions for those who defame Jewish people under the guise of 'Palestinian advocacy'."
Justice Papageorgiou cautioned against reading larger messages into DeLuca's win, noting in her judgment that defamation suits are meant to "vindicate reputation," not as a "tool to fight racism, antisemitism or other kinds of prejudice."
And DeLuca was indeed made whole. But how, in the present circumstances, could this case not punch above its weight? If antisemitism was a serious problem before Oct. 7 - and it was - it was a mostly submerged iceberg in terms of public perception. Now the massive iceberg is on full display in all its staggering weight.
Nevertheless, defamation suits on this file cannot treat antisemitism's root causes; they can only treat its symptoms. As one huge root cause of flagrant antisemitism, the universities have long cried out for a reckoning. Fortunately, the current ferment in the U.S. over Harvard University and other Ivy League antisemitism enablers is also bubbling up here.
The Lawfare Project recently partnered with the law firm of Diamond and Diamond personal injuries Lawyers to file a $77 million class action suit against McMaster University and the McMaster Students' Union. A Jan. 3 statement of claim alleges that "McMaster University has become, for over a decade, home to systemic discrimination, harassment, hatred, antisemitism, violent discourse, and actual or threatened physical harm amongst the student body." Plaintiffs are all currently enrolled students and those who graduated within the last two years.
In fact, Diamond and Diamond already have five other prominent universities (plus many of their student unions) - Queen's, York, Concordia, Toronto Metropolitain and the University of British Columbia - in their proposed crosshairs on the same grounds. Diamonds'managing partner Sandra Zisckind told CTV news, "You should not be allowed to bring the Hamas flag to a university." As for free speech rights, "You can disagree with Israel, you can say there needs to be a ceasefire, those are things that are tantamount to free speech, but you cannot say we need to kill the Jews."
If the suits go forward - none of the claims have been certified by the courts - a win would be tantamount to an immediately brutal, but ultimately healing form of chemotherapy on a long-metastizing social cancer. A loss? Let's not even go there.
National Post kaybarb@gmail.com X.com/BarbaraRKay

Document 33 of 104

When conservatives 'pounce'; The right finds its cautionary tale of subtle media bias

Author: Thomson, Stuart

Publication info: Thomson, Stuart. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/when-conservatives-pounce-right-finds-cautionary/docview/2913777202/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Apq1busgeneral&amp;atitle=When+conservatives+%27pounce%27%3B+The+right+finds+its+cautionary+tale+of+subtle+media+bias&amp;title=National+Post&amp;issn=14868008&amp;date=2024-01-13&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.5&amp;au=Thomson%2C+Stuart&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=National+Post&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Accusations of liberal media bias usually come in two distinct flavours.
There are boisterous complaints about "Justin journos" sucking up to the government, purveying "fake news" and peddling lies for partisan and ideological purposes.
There is also a more subtle complaint about the media: that reporters tend to personally lean left in such numbers that the press as a whole lacks ideological diversity and struggles to understand the politics of the right.
It's clear that both views have gone mainstream in recent years as qualms over media reliability have morphed into widespread mistrust among Canadians. Abacus Data found in 2022 that 44 per cent of Canadians believe "much of the information" they receive from media outlets is false.
And the more conservative you are, the less likely you are to trust the media.
Only 31 per cent of Liberal voters say information they get from the media is false, compared to 59 per cent of Conservatives. An astonishing 89 per cent of People's Party of Canada voters say they don't trust the media. Plunging faith in the media also coincides with government efforts to help beleaguered news organizations adapt to a digital age that has disrupted their ad revenue.
Critics on the right, including federal Conservative Leader Pierre Poilievre, have argued that the hundreds of millions of dollars in Liberal newsroom bailouts give journalists a vested interested in protecting the government. (The same charge sticks even more naturally to the predominantly government-funded CBC.)
Even those conservatives who don't think journalists are undercover partisan operators sometimes still detect a subtle type of bias in the news, which may even be more pernicious.
It's not that reporters are putting their finger on the scales for a particular political party, necessarily. But they believe journalists are steeped in a centre-left worldview and exhibit a kind of unconscious bias that gently but consistently tilts the coverage against conservatives.
"I can think of no reporters I've ever dealt with who had it out for a party or a worldview. Most were trying to do the job in the fairest way possible," said Andrew MacDougall, who was director of communications for former prime minister Stephen Harper and is now a director at Trafalgar Strategy.
"But like all biases, you're not really aware of them until somebody points them out. If you're university-educated and urban-living, you tend to have a worldview that is different from somebody who isn't - and it takes a lot of effort to open your eyes up," said MacDougall.
The perceived bias can be so subtle that it can be difficult to articulate. Critics might discern it from the tone of a story, which is subjective and tough to exhibit as ironclad proof of bias. Sometimes it's in the magnitude of the coverage given to progressive issues over others (think: climate versus the economy), and the type of sources that are chosen to comment on the stories.
Every now and then, though, conservatives spot a smoking gun they can wave around as irrefutable proof of the subterranean bias they see in the media.
When former Harvard president Claudine Gay resigned after a catastrophic appearance in front of Congress in December and a flurry of subsequent plagiarism allegations, conservatives were only mildly surprised to find that much of the news coverage blamed them for the president's missteps.
One headline, from The Associated Press, rocketed around right-winger group chats and attracted an avalanche of ire on social media: "Harvard president's resignation highlights new conservative weapon against colleges: plagiarism."
It exemplified perfectly for conservatives the understated kind of media bias they see around them - one so obvious that it could serve as a case study that anyone could understand.
"Part of why the AP story was so gobsmacking was because it was so shamelessly not aligned with common sense. If you're just a regular person who doesn't care a lot about politics and you see that headline, you go, 'Wait, what? When did plagiarism become a political issue?'" said longtime conservative strategist Ginny Roth, a partner at Crestview Strategy.
It's a long-running inside joke in American conservative circles that when Republicans have a scandal, the press writes about the scandal, but when Democrats have a scandal, the press reports how Republicans "pounce" on the scandal. The phenomenon even has its own Wikipedia page (titled "Republicans pounce," of course) and some pundits make a point of keeping a running registry of these kinds of stories.
National Review writer Charles C.W. Cooke attributes mainstream press biases to his belief that journalists naturally tend to agree with left-of-centre policies and politicians. Even when they attempt to maintain objectivity, it affects the framing of their stories.
"Almost invariably, the press assumes that what the Democrats are doing is normal and that what Republicans are doing is not - even when it is the Democrats who are proposing big changes. This formulation always results in the GOP being cast as the aggressor," writes Cooke.
In recent decades, conservative parties have begun working to circumvent mainstream journalists as the filter for their messages, with strategists taking the same view of journalists having leftward inclinations that affect their coverage. Conservative Leader Pierre Poilievre has prioritized video messages and other ways of directly reaching out to Canadians and, when he does talk to the media, he's not afraid to tussle with them.
"Pierre Poilievre has made a very strong argument for speaking directly to people and basically avoiding the media as much as possible. And who can blame him?" said Roth. "The more you see headlines like (the AP story), the more I think average Canadians don't give him any grief for not doing constant waste-of-time interviews with a mainstream media that is predisposed to oppose everything he stands for and that isn't even reflective of the views of mainstream Canadians."
Research about the ideological leanings of journalists suggests that view might not be entirely off-base.
Journalism today draws more heavily than ever from highly-educated urbanites. And those groups are far more likely to hold progressive views than a general sample of the population.
Research in the United States also found that reporters overwhelmingly describe themselves as liberal.
A survey of 462 financial journalists found that 58 per cent described themselves as very or somewhat liberal, while only four per cent described their views as very or somewhat conservative, reported a 2018 paper by researchers at the Arizona State University and Texas A&M University.
A recent study by The Economist, analyzing the language used by prominent outlets between 2017 and 2022, found that mainstream news generally veered to the left.
The study found that 17 of the 20 most-read news websites in the U.S. used more language associated with the left than the right. It found that TV news outlets, like CNN, underwent a bigger shift to the left over the five years than did written media.
"This Democratic slant has grown over time, driven mainly by changes in once-centrist outlets," reports The Economist.
The reasons for this are debated, but one theory is that it was another effect from the broader reckoning around social justice that happened during the pandemic and in the wake of the George Floyd killing. Institutions from retailers to museums began explicitly espousing or supporting views associated with left-wing ideology, like critical race theory. Newsrooms were not immune to the pull.
"It used to be that newsrooms were biased towards a small-L liberal, centrist consensus and not that representative of fringe views. Now, I think they're representative actually of pretty farleft fringe views," said Roth.
While MacDougall agrees conservatives generally have to work harder to get a fair shake from the media, he also warned people on the right not to get too caught up in playing the victim. He pointed to the current controversy around Prime Minister Justin Trudeau's recent free luxury holiday in Jamaica as proof that, above all, the media craves juicy stories.
And when conservatives govern, he said, there are times when the negative reporting targeting them is just proper reporting.
"The hardest thing in government to do is to distinguish between the fact that you're getting heat for being in government versus for being the party that you are in government," he said.
National Post

January 12, 2024 (Page A4)

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Apdnnationalpost&amp;atitle=January+12%2C+2024+%28Page+A4%29&amp;title=National+Post+%282011-%29&amp;issn=14868008&amp;date=2024-01-12&amp;volume=26&amp;issue=64&amp;spage=A4&amp;au=&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=National+Post+%282011-%29&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

ProQuest document ID: 2917446101

Document 40 of 104

A Wilfrid Laurier professor tweeted about Hamas. One student is trying to get her disciplined

Author: Dawson, Tyler

Publication info: Dawson, Tyler. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/blogs-podcasts-websites/wilfrid-laurier-professor-tweeted-about-hamas-one/docview/2913691391/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Apq1busgeneral&amp;atitle=A+Wilfrid+Laurier+professor+tweeted+about+Hamas.+One+student+is+trying+to+get+her+disciplined&amp;title=National+Post+%28Online%29&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-12&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=Dawson%2C+Tyler&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=National+Post+%28Online%29&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Wilfrid Laurier University, which has adopted a free speech policy that allows for "difficult, controversial, extreme, or even wrong-headed" ideas on campus, has seemingly not taken any action to discipline a professor who called for the support of Palestinians on the morning of the Oct. 7 massacre in Israel.
Jessica Hutchison, who focuses "on researching and disrupting racism, settler colonialism and gendered state violence," particularly in prisons, tweeted that day about land acknowledgements - now often standard practice before official events - saying that they should "include support for Palestinians who are taking their land back from settler colonizers."
Critics took that as an endorsement of the Hamas terrorists who crossed Israel's border with Gaza on Oct. 7, killing 1,200 Israelis and kidnapping 240 others.
Hutchison subsequently locked her Twitter account.
John Findlay, who's studying criminology at Wilfrid Laurier University, wrote in late October to the Office of Human Rights and Conflict Management that he was "profoundly and seriously disturbed by the post."
"Professor Hutchinson's post cannot be justified as a legitimate exercise of freedom of speech or academic discourse. It violates an inviolable Canadian principle - that the murder of innocent people cannot be tolerated or promoted," Findlay wrote.
In the letter, he argues that Hutchison's post was antisemitic and discriminatory, shows a lack of empathy for victims of the violence, may constitute participating in terrorism, encourages violence and dehumanizes Israelis.
"I would call upon Professor Hutchinson to disavow her post with a clear and unambiguous apology. I would further expect the university to publicly condemn the post in the strongest language," Findlay wrote.
In an interview, Findlay said the university has yet to respond.
"Basically, I've been told nothing," he said.
After the National Post made multiple requests for comment, Vanessa Barrasa, the university's director of communications and issues management, said "Laurier is not commenting on this matter."
So far, it does not appear that Wilfrid Laurier University acceded to Findlay's demands. An email chain obtained by the Post between Findlay and the university says "there will be no further action from this office with regard to the issues you have raised."
On Oct. 12, more than a week before Findlay sent his letter to the human rights office, he wrote to university president Deborah MacLatchy, asking whether there would be a public response to Hutchison's post.
"As an institute of higher learning, Laurier supports the expression of a range of perspectives, including those that may be deemed difficult, controversial, or extreme," said a reply from the president's office. "At the same time, it's important to note that the opinions of individual members of the Laurier community do not necessarily reflect the views and values of the university."
A further email to Findlay, this time from the Office of Student Affairs, reiterated the university's commitment to free expression, but also said "we strongly condemn acts of antisemitism and Islamophobia and have made it clear to our community we have zero tolerance for hate speech, racism, threats, and harassment."
Hutchison, who is teaching a course on social policy and activism in the winter 2024 term, did not return the Post's request for comment.
It is unclear whether Hutchison was spoken to formally or informally or why her tweet would not fall - if indeed it does not - under the university's harassment and discrimination policies. Findlay has yet to make a formal discrimination complaint about Hutchison's tweet.
"They've discovered freedom of speech," Findlay said, wryly. "Free speech has its limits. You can't call for the invocation of murder against somebody. That's not free speech."
The use of the term "settler" to refer to Israelis has been controversial. When founded in 1949, Israel was situated on the Biblical homeland of the Jewish people, which was around two-thirds Palestinian. Jewish immigration had increased considerably after the First World War, leading to conflict between Zionists and the Arab population. The land itself had been under various rulers over the centuries, whether Bronze Age Jewish rule, or as a component of the Byzantine, Roman or Islamic empires.
For some Palestinians, the land Israel stands upon is their homeland; several hundred thousand were expelled from their homes in Israel's early years, in what Palestinians call the Nakba. Israelis, meanwhile, argue that it is their historic homeland. Max Nordau, one of the founders of the Zionist Organization, who died more than two decades before Israel became a reality, wrote in The Arabs and Us that Jews had a right to their "legal and historical inheritance, the land of their fathers, of which they were robbed 1,900 years ago by the Roman aggressors."
Today, roughly one-quarter of Israeli citizens were born in other countries but have since settled in Israel, moving to the country under the auspices of the 1950 Law of Return, which allows for any person with one or more Jewish grandparent to come and live in the country.
More broadly, the issue of campus free speech and academic freedom has been a topic of debate in recent years. Indeed, much of the controversy originated with Wilfrid Laurier University. In 2017, Lindsay Shepherd, then a teaching assistant and now a media commentator at True North Centre, was accused of creating a "toxic climate for some of the students" when she showed an interview with psychologist Jordan Peterson to her students.
The university ordered an investigation and found, in the words of university president MacLatchy, that there had been "no wrongdoing on the part of Ms. Shepherd in showing the clip from TVO in her tutorial."
In 2018, Doug Ford's Progressive Conservative government required Ontario universities to adopt stricter policies to protect free speech on campus, similar to the Chicago Principles, which state that institutions of higher education "should not attempt to shield students from ideas or opinions that they disagree with or find offensive," and that members of a university or college community "may not obstruct or interfere with the freedom of others to express their views."
Wilfrid Laurier University adopted such a policy in 2019.
"The university supports the expression, testing, and challenging of a range of perspectives and ideas, including those that may be deemed difficult, controversial, extreme, or even wrong-headed," its free speech statement says.

Document 41 of 104

FIRST READING: The evidence for paid protesters in the Canadian anti-Israel camp

Author: Hopper, Tristin

Publication info: Hopper, Tristin. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/blogs-podcasts-websites/first-reading-evidence-paid-protesters-canadian/docview/2913665187/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Apq1busgeneral&amp;atitle=FIRST+READING%3A+The+evidence+for+paid+protesters+in+the+Canadian+anti-Israel+camp&amp;title=National+Post+%28Online%29&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-12&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=Hopper%2C+Tristin&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=National+Post+%28Online%29&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: First Reading is a daily newsletter keeping you posted on the travails of Canadian politicos, all curated by the National Post's own Tristin Hopper. To get an early version sent directly to your inbox, sign uphere.
TOP STORY
After weeks of hosting sustained anti-Israel demonstrations in its downtown, the B.C. capital of Victoria has recently played host to controversial revelations that paid protesters could be padding the ranks of the Canadian anti-Israel movement.
The Plenty Collective - a self-described "mutual aid group" that has previously received civic grant funding - was found to be operating a "Solidarity Fund" to distribute cash to anyone "organizing actions" related to "Palestine and Palestinian people."
Details of the fund, which are still on the official Plenty Collective website as of press time, were circulated earlier this week by Ian Ward, a councillor for the Victoria-area municipality of Colwood.
"Paid 'protestors' promoting antisemitism & call for violent intifada," wrote Ward in a social media post
The pro-Palestine crowd in Victoria is paying for underemployed rabble to join their mob!
Paid "protestors" promoting antisemitism & call for violent intifada. Only in #victoriabc ?? @jfcuia @kinsellawarren @globeandmail @nationalpost @StopAntisemites
- Ian (@ianincolwood) January 9, 2024
Recipients of Solidarity Fund monies need not even provide their name. Applicants are required only to submit details of their race (in order to "prioritize Palestinian and BIPOC applicants") and a brief description of how they intend to use the cash.
"This can include, but is not limited to, the costs of lost wages, supplies, items for fundraising, paying speakers, etc.," it reads.
The Plenty Collective was a recent co-recipient of $28,000 distributed as part of a 2023 Community Grants Program overseen by the Victoria Foundation. In a Tuesday statement, the Victoria Foundation say it was reviewing the donation.
"As a funder, it is our expectation that grants are used for the purposes outlined in the application, and any changes to that need to be raised with the Foundation," it read
While the level of activism in Victoria hasn't been to the scale or severity of what's been occurring in Central Canada, since Oct. 7 the city has witnessed regular demonstrations centred around the B.C. legislature.
"Their language has been threatening, with chants for Intifada and slogans that call for the end of Israel," read a recent op-ed by Victoria Rabbi Lynn Greenhough in the Victoria Times Colonist.
In the article, Greenhough detailed how she had publicly planned to personally stand in the way of an anti-Israel car rally planned for Dec. 31 - leading to a call from a Victoria police constable warning her that it was illegal to block a roadway.
"When I pointed out that over the past months Victoria roadways were blocked on a weekly basis, she replied, 'We can't arrest 500 people,'" wrote Greenhough.
Trudeau government declaring its position on prosecuting Israel for 'genocide': Here's what to know
FIRST READING: The Canadian military's all-in embrace of far-left 'anti-oppression' dogma
Victoria also happens to be home of Susan Kim, a city councillor notable as one of only two elected Canadian politicians to sign a November petition dismissing widespread evidence that Hamas terrorists committed mass-rape on Oct. 7. The other politician signatory was Sarah Jama, an Ontario MPP who was kicked out of the Ontario NDP after justifying the massacres as an act of decolonization.
While the Plenty Collective has yielded the most brazen example of funds being distributed to anti-Israel demonstrators, similar charges have emerged in Montreal.
In December, Postmedia columnist Warren Kinsella published allegations from Montreal pro-Israel journalist Beryl Wajsman that activists had divided the city into a grid system, with paid captains coordinating the demonstrations in each grid.
SURJ Toronto, a far-left "anti-racist" collective, has solicited donations for a "Community Defence Fund" to support the local chapter of the Palestinian Youth Movement - a perennial organizer of anti-Israel rallies, including a series of celebratory demonstrations held within hours of the Oct. 7 massacres.
According to Community Defence Fund's official website, it has amassed $78,071 as of press time.
Fundraising is helped by the fact that some of the key players in Canada's anti-Israel movement have non-profit status.
This is most notably true of Samidoun, a Vancouver-based organization with strong links to the Popular Front for the Liberation of Palestine, a Gaza terror group believed to have participated in the Oct. 7 massacres. Both Germany and Israel list Samidoun as a terrorist entity, and research by Israel's Ministry of Strategic Affairs has previously claimed it is "likely" that some of the group's Canadian donations are finding their way to the PFLP.
Long before Oct. 7, Samidoun (and its non-profit status) was the subject of an official request for information by Conservative MP Melissa Lantsman, who petitioned the minister of public safety as to why it hadn't been denied certification due to its links to terror.
A reply from the ministry, issued in Nov. 2022, said that "the Government of Canada takes terrorist threats against Canada and its citizens seriously."
The Ministry of Public Safety's official reply to a question of whether Samidoun was being monitored as a potential terrorist threat.
Non-profit status has also been extended to Palesign, one of the central groups in organizing an extended blockade of the Avenue Road Bridge - a freeway overpass adjoining one of Canada's most Jewish neighbourhoods.
Palesign has no records or web presence prior to Oct. 7, indicating that it was likely founded and certified as a non-profit entity for the specific purpose of organizing anti-Israel actions in relation to the current Israel-Hamas conflict.
IN OTHER NEWS
Speaking of the Avenue Road Bridge, the Toronto police have finally announced that they're going to start arresting people who try to blockade it. This is after nearly two weeks of police observing and even helping to facilitate an extended anti-Israel blockade of the overpass, which services one of Canada's most conspicuously Jewish neighbourhoods. Most notoriously, this included the above image, captured by protest-watcher Caryma S'ad, of police delivering coffee and donuts to blockaders (the police hadn't purchased the coffee, but still).
As has been frequently claimed in this newsletter, it only requires basic arithmetic to show that Canada's decision to dial up immigration to unprecedented highs is negatively affecting housing affordability. To wit, Canada builds about 200,000 new homes a year, and is now taking in upwards of one million people annually. The Canadian Press has just uncovered documents showing that federal public servants were openly warning the Trudeau government that sky-high immigration would worsen housing affordability. In 2022, internal reports stated starkly that "population growth has exceeded the growth in available housing units" - and that this would get worse if the feds stayed the course on plans to raise immigration to 500,000 per year. The warning was ignored, of course, which is probably why average rents are continuing to increase by about $100 each month.
Ed Broadbent has just died at age 87. He was the leader of the NDP for 14 years between 1975 and 1989, during which time the party was able to incrementally benefit from plummeting faith in the Liberals. When Broadbent took over, the NDP caucus stood at 17 seats. At his last federal election as leader in 1988, that total would get to 43. He's pictured above after winning a seat in Ottawa Centre in 2004, in what would turn out to be a two-year return to federal politics done on the invitation of then-leader Jack Layton.
The International Court of Justice began hearings this week over South Africa's claim that Israel's stated intention to destroy Hamas constitutes genocide. Spoiler alert: It's an incredibly specious claim brought by a South African government that has long been an apologist for Palestinian extremism (they're one of the world's only governments to have opened diplomatic ties with Hamas, for one). But the proceedings have nevertheless divided the Liberal caucus. Salma Zahid - who has previously organized "Palestinian solidarity" events on Parliament Hill that ended up including pro-terror extremists on the guest list - came out in support of the South African charge So did Liberal MP Iqra Khalid And on the other side are Anthony Housefather and Marco Mendicino, who released a statement calling the South African charges "baseless and unconscionable."

Document 42 of 104

Anthony Furey: Olivia Chow's tax hike would be unnecessary and destructive

Author: to, Special

Publication info: to, Special. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/blogs-podcasts-websites/anthony-furey-olivia-chows-tax-hike-would-be/docview/2913647309/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Apq1busgeneral&amp;atitle=Anthony+Furey%3A+Olivia+Chow%27s+tax+hike+would+be+unnecessary+and+destructive&amp;title=National+Post+%28Online%29&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-12&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=to%2C+Special&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=National+Post+%28Online%29&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Olivia Chow had a choice. Toronto's Mayor could have rolled up her sleeves and got to work going through the City's budget to find savings and hold the line on excessive cost increases. Instead, she chose to grow the budget by a billion dollars and then push for one of the largest tax increases in recent city history right when regular folks are facing an affordability crisis. It's a heartless thing to do given the state of the economy.
On Wednesday, Chow proposed a 10.5 per cent property tax increase for the coming year. This will climb to 16.5 per cent if the federal government declines to provide Chow with the refugee housing funds she's requested.
It would be one thing if Chow was bringing in this big ticket tax increase as a measure of last resort. But she has made no meaningful attempt at finding savings and efficiencies or performing any sort of spending or service review. In fact, Mayor Chow and Budget Chief Shelley Carroll actually voted against a service review a few months ago.
This is a shame. Toronto is long overdue for a full and proper budget inspection.
The City of Toronto's operating budget has almost doubled in the past decade. It went from $9.6 billion in 2014 to the current $17 billion. Last year it was $16 billion, but Chow has added $1 billion right out of the gate.
People already feel like they aren't getting good value for money. Given the state of congestion and infrastructure in this city, it feels like we're receiving less, not more, when it comes to municipal services.
When it comes to policing, we definitely are getting less. Crime and public safety is one of the top concerns voiced by residents and yet we have several hundred fewer frontline officers than we did a decade ago, and that's to serve a much larger population. Now, we will pay even more but still get less.
Keep in mind, this massive tax hike comes after Chow struck a recent deal with Ontario Premier Doug Ford that saw the province contribute an extra $400 million for the year. Instead of using this money to balance the books, Chow appears to have put it towards her $1 billion spending splurge.
"The City has done its part to address opening pressures of $1.776 billion," the budget documents proclaim. They don't offer any evidence to back the statement up though. And now, Chow is passing the buck to the taxpayers to insist that they do her job of balancing the books.
This means the average household can expect to pay around $500 more per year in housing to cover these taxes. While homeowners will pay upfront on their property taxes, renters will also shoulder this burden, as this increase will be passed on to them via higher rents.
Make no mistake about it, these taxes are part of the cost of housing and Canada is in the midst of a housing crisis. Toronto residents are facing the worst of the crisis.
We also can't tax our way to prosperity. If Chow takes more of people's money without proposing any credible plan to responsibly manage the budget, that will only make the city a less attractive place to invest and do business. We can't share the wealth if we don't first grow the wealth.
But Chow has clearly made her choice. She's taking the reckless tax and spend route. Now it's up to members of council and city residents to make their choice. Will they let this budget stand or will they push for common sense amendments? Let's hope they pick the latter.
Residents and businesses can participate in town halls, speak at the budget committee and, of course, send feedback to City Hall. Councillors get to grill the Mayor and then decide whether to vote for or against this massive tax increase. They should definitely not vote for it as is.
The people of Toronto deserve better and there are so many tools in the budget toolkit that can still be used. A meaningful line-by-line review is the most obvious. But we can also make use of attrition, to reduce the public service headcount without laying anyone off. We can institute a non-core services hiring freeze. We can separate programs into "need to haves" versus "nice to haves" and allocate resources accordingly. The list goes on.
Nobody said managing the city budget would be easy. But jacking up people's taxes like this? Now that's taking the easy way out.
This double digit tax increase will be remembered for a long time to come, especially by those struggling to pay the bills.
Anthony Furey is the Executive Director of The Stronach Foundation for Economic Rights. He ran for mayor of Toronto in the 2023 by-election.

Document 43 of 104

When 'conservatives pounce': The right finds its cautionary tale of subtle media bias

Author: Thomson, Stuart

Publication info: Thomson, Stuart. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/blogs-podcasts-websites/when-conservatives-pounce-right-finds-cautionary/docview/2913647060/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Apq1busgeneral&amp;atitle=When+%27conservatives+pounce%27%3A+The+right+finds+its+cautionary+tale+of+subtle+media+bias&amp;title=National+Post+%28Online%29&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-12&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=Thomson%2C+Stuart&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=National+Post+%28Online%29&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: OTTAWA - Accusations of liberal media bias usually come in two distinct flavours.
There are boisterous complaints about "Justin journos" sucking up to the government, purveying "fake news" and peddling lies for partisan and ideological purposes.
There is also a more subtle complaint about the media: that reporters tend to personally lean left in such numbers that the press as a whole lacks ideological diversity and struggles to understand the politics of the right.
It's clear that both views have gone mainstream in recent years as qualms over media reliability have morphed into widespread mistrust among Canadians. Abacus Data found in 2022 that 44 per cent of Canadians believe "much of the information" they receive from media outlets is false.
And the more conservative you are, the less you are likely to trust the media.
Only 31 per cent of Liberal voters say that information they get from the media is false, compared to 59 per cent of Conservatives. An astonishing 89 per cent of People's Party of Canada voters say they don't trust the media. Plunging faith in the media also coincides with government efforts to help beleaguered news organizations adapt to a digital age that has disrupted their ad revenue.
Critics on the right, including federal Conservative Leader Pierre Poilievre, have argued that the hundreds of millions of dollars in Liberal newsroom bailouts give journalists a vested interested in protecting the government. (The same charge sticks even more naturally to the predominantly government-funded CBC.)
Even those conservatives who don't think journalists are undercover partisan operators sometimes still detect a subtle type of bias in the news, which may even be more pernicious.
It's not that reporters are putting their finger on the scales for a particular political party, necessarily. But they believe journalists are steeped in a centre-left worldview and exhibit a kind of unconscious bias that gently but consistently tilts the coverage against conservatives.
"I can think of no reporters I've ever dealt with who had it out for a party or a worldview. Most were trying to do the job in the fairest way possible," said Andrew MacDougall, who was director of communications for former prime minister Stephen Harper and is now a director at Trafalgar Strategy.
"But like all biases, you're not really aware of them until somebody points them out. If you're university-educated and urban-living, you tend to have a worldview that is different from somebody who isn't - and it takes a lot of effort to open your eyes up," said MacDougall.
The perceived bias can be so subtle that it can be difficult to articulate. Critics might discern it from the tone of a story, which is subjective and tough to exhibit as ironclad proof of bias. Sometimes it's in the magnitude of the coverage given to progressive issues over others (think: climate versus the economy), and the type of sources that are chosen to comment on the stories.
Every now and then, though, conservatives spot a smoking gun they can wave around as irrefutable proof of the subterranean bias they see in the media.
When former Harvard president Claudine Gay resigned after a catastrophic appearance in front of Congress in December and a flurry of subsequent plagiarism allegations, conservatives were only mildly surprised to find that much of the news coverage blamed them for the president's missteps.
One headline, from The Associated Press, rocketed around right-winger group chats and attracted an avalanche of ire on social media: "Harvard president's resignation highlights new conservative weapon against colleges: plagiarism."
Harvard University president Claudine Gay speaks during a hearing of the U.S. House Committee on Education on Dec. 5, 2023, in Washington. Gay resigned Jan. 2, 2024, amid plagiarism accusations and criticism over her remarks regarding antisemitism.
It exemplified perfectly for conservatives the understated kind of media bias they see around them - one so obvious that it could serve as a case study that anyone could understand.
"Part of why the AP story was so gobsmacking was because it was so shamelessly not aligned with common sense. If you're just a regular person who doesn't care a lot about politics and you see that headline, you go, 'Wait, what? When did plagiarism become a political issue?'" said long-time conservative strategist Ginny Roth, a partner at Crestview Strategy.
It's a long-running inside joke in American conservative circles that when Republicans have a scandal, the press writes about the scandal, but when Democrats have a scandal, the press reports how Republicans "pounce" on the scandal. The phenomenon even has its own Wikipedia page (titled "Republicans pounce," of course) and some pundits make a point of keeping a running registry of these kinds of stories.
National Review writer Charles C.W. Cooke attributes mainstream press biases to his belief that journalists naturally tend to agree with left-of-centre policies and politicians. Even when they attempt to maintain objectivity, it affects the framing of their stories.
"Almost invariably, the press assumes that what the Democrats are doing is normal and that what Republicans are doing is not - even when it is the Democrats who are proposing big changes. This formulation always results in the GOP being cast as the aggressor," writes Cooke.
In recent decades, conservative parties have begun working to circumvent mainstream journalists as the filter for their messages, with strategists taking the same view of journalists having leftward inclinations that affect their coverage. Conservative leader Pierre Poilievre has prioritized video messages and other ways of directly reaching out to Canadians and, when he does talk to the media, he's not afraid to tussle with them
"Pierre Poilievre has made a very strong argument for speaking directly to people and basically avoiding the media as much as possible. And who can blame him?" said Roth. "The more you see headlines like (the AP story), the more I think average Canadians don't give him any grief for not doing constant waste-of-time interviews with a mainstream media that is predisposed to oppose everything he stands for and that isn't even reflective of the views of mainstream Canadians."
Poilievre's Conservatives are using the 'selfie' video to remake the Canadian political conversation
Colby Cosh: Reporter in shambles as Poilievre dismantles loaded questioning with ease
Research about the ideological leanings of journalists suggests that view might not be entirely off-base.
Journalism today draws more heavily than ever from highly-educated urbanites. And those groups are far more likely to hold progressive views than a general sample of the population.
Research in the United States also found that reporters overwhelmingly describe themselves as liberal.
A survey of 462 financial journalists found that 58 per cent described themselves as very or somewhat liberal, while only four per cent described their views as very or somewhat conservative, reported a 2018 paper by researchers at the Arizona State University and Texas A&M University.
A recent study by The Economist, analyzing the language used by prominent outlets between 2017 and 2022, found that mainstream news generally veered to the left.
The study found that 17 of the 20 most-read news websites in the U.S. used more language associated with the left than the right. It found that TV news outlets, like CNN, underwent a bigger shift to the left over the five years than did written media.
"This Democratic slant has grown over time, driven mainly by changes in once-centrist outlets," reports The Economist.
The reasons for this are debated, but one theory is that it was another effect from the broader reckoning around social justice that happened during the pandemic and in the wake of the George Floyd killing Institutions from retailers to museums began explicitly espousing or supporting views associated with left-wing ideology, like critical race theory. Newsrooms were not immune to the pull.
"It used to be that newsrooms were biased towards a small-L liberal, centrist consensus and not that representative of fringe views. Now, I think they're representative actually of pretty far-left fringe views," said Roth.
While MacDougall agrees that conservatives generally have to work harder to get a fair shake from the media, he also warned people on the right not to get too caught up in playing the victim. He pointed to the current controversy around Prime Minister Justin Trudeau's recent free luxury holiday in Jamaica as proof that, above all, the media craves juicy stories.
And when conservatives govern, he said, there are times when the negative reporting targeting them is just proper reporting.
"The hardest thing in government to do is to distinguish between the fact that you're getting heat for being in government versus for being the party that you are in government," he said.
National Post

Document 44 of 104

A cure for paperwork and a fix for Ontario's family doctor crisis

Author: Denley, Randall

Publication info: Denley, Randall. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/cure-paperwork-fix-ontarios-family-doctor-crisis/docview/2913580072/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Apq1busgeneral&amp;atitle=A+cure+for+paperwork+and+a+fix+for+Ontario%27s+family+doctor+crisis&amp;title=National+Post&amp;issn=14868008&amp;date=2024-01-12&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.5&amp;au=Denley%2C+Randall&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=National+Post&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Right now, 2.3 million Ontarians have no family doctor according to the Ontario College of Family Physicians. By 2026, the college expects that number to nearly double to 4.4 million.
This is a situation that undermines the foundation of public health care. Without a family doctor, Ontarians are disconnected from the health-care system and denied service they have paid for through their taxes. People who can't get timely primary care fill up hospital emergency rooms or, worse, they don't get treatment at all and just get sicker.
No one is immune. Even if you have a family doctor today, what happens when that person retires? Overnight, you go from having primary care to waiting at the end of an enormous and ever-lengthening queue.
The magnitude of the problem demands a bold stroke from the Ontario government. The good news is that there is a potential solution, one that could be implemented within a year. First, some background.
The primary-care shortage is a problem with a long history. Back in 2016, 1.3 million Ontarians had no family doctor. The figure was considered alarming. By 2018, it had risen to 1.8 million. The number today is somewhat in dispute. The government says there are 1.2 million people without a doctor. Either way, it's a big problem.
The causes of the doctor shortage are no secret.
Ontario's population is growing far faster than its supply of doctors, many physicians are hitting retirement age, and new graduates have been reluctant to enter family medicine.
Last year, Ontario added 500,000 people. The province's supply of active family physicians increased by an average of just over 200 a year for the last five years according to the Ontario Physician Reporting Centre. Family doctors have 1,200 patients on average. At the current rate of population growth, new doctor supply will only meet half the new demand much less put a dent in the family doctor shortfall.
This is not a problem that can be fixed by the incremental changes the Doug Ford government has announced, things like adding more places in medical schools or creating a few more interdisciplinary team practices to help support doctors. The way to fix the problem is to understand the overlap between what doctors want and what patients need.
The major issue for family doctors, according to their college, is the amount of time they have to spend on paperwork, things like writing sick notes, filling in insurance forms and trying to find specialists. Dr. Mekalai Kumanan, president of the family physicians'college, says the paperwork burden takes up to 19 hours of the average doctor's work week, about 40 per cent of their work hours. Not only is the paperwork time consuming, it's frustrating for doctors who got into medicine to treat patients, not fill in forms. The red tape associated with family medicine leads to burnout and deters young doctors from joining the specialty, Kumanan said in an interview.
Ontario has added more residency spaces for family doctors, but the government is fighting a trend. Increasingly, family medicine graduates don't go on to establish a family medicine practice, choosing instead to run sports medicine clinics or work shifts in hospitals. In 2008, 77 per cent of family doctors worked in their traditional role. By 2022, the number had dropped to 65 per cent, the family medicine college says.
What the government should do is improve the productivity of the family doctors Ontario already has, taking away as much of the administrative burden as possible. In exchange, doctors should be expected to accept more patients.
The numbers would look something like this: Ontario has 14,368 family doctors in active practice, the provincial government says. Taking on all of the 2.3 million people who lack a doctor would require each doctor to add 160 new patients, thus increasing the average practice size by about 13 per cent.
There would be costs to this fix. The government would be paying for these new administrators, and doctors who receive a base fee per patient in their practice would earn more for doing more.
In return, 2.3 million Ontarians would finally start to get the care they need, pressure would be taken off other parts of the health system, the field of family medicine would be made more attractive, and each new graduate could take on a larger practice.
That would be a big gain for patients, for doctors, and for politicians looking for a major health care win to talk about during the next election. That sure beats the status quo.
National Post Randall Denley is an Ottawa journalist, author and former Ontario PC candidate. Contact him at randalldenley1@gmail.com

Document 45 of 104

Evidence found of 'paid protesters' in the Canadian anti-Israel camp; Non-profits using donations to organize

Author: Hopper, Tristin

Publication info: Hopper, Tristin. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/evidence-found-paid-protesters-canadian-anti/docview/2913579968/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Apq1busgeneral&amp;atitle=Evidence+found+of+%27paid+protesters%27+in+the+Canadian+anti-Israel+camp%3B+Non-profits+using+donations+to+organize&amp;title=National+Post&amp;issn=14868008&amp;date=2024-01-12&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.4&amp;au=Hopper%2C+Tristin&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=National+Post&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: After weeks of hosting anti-Israel demonstrations in its downtown, the B.C. capital of Victoria has recently played host to controversial revelations that paid protesters could be padding the ranks of the Canadian anti-Israel movement.
The Plenty Collective - a self-described "mutual aid group" that has received civic funding - was found to be operating a "Solidarity Fund" to distribute cash to anyone "organizing actions" related to "Palestine and Palestinian people."
Details of the fund, which were still on the official Plenty Collective website as of press time, were circulated earlier this week by Ian Ward, a councillor for the Victoria-area municipality of Colwood.
"Paid 'protestors'promoting antisemitism & call for violent intifada," wrote Ward in a social media post.
Recipients of Solidarity Fund monies need not even provide their name. Applicants are required only to submit details of their race (in order to "prioritize Palestinian and BIPOC applicants") and a brief description of how they intend to use the cash.
"This can include, but is not limited to, the costs of lost wages, supplies, items for fundraising, paying speakers, etc.," it reads.
The Plenty Collective was a recent co-recipient of $28,000 distributed as part of a 2023 Community Grants Program overseen by the Victoria Foundation. In a statement this week, the Victoria Foundation says it is reviewing the donation.
"As a funder, it is our expectation that grants are used for the purposes outlined in the application, and any changes to that need to be raised with the Foundation," it read.
While the level of activism in Victoria hasn't been to the scale of what's been occurring in Central Canada since Israel declared war on Hamas on Oct. 7, the city has witnessed regular demonstrations centred around the B.C. legislature.
"Their language has been threatening, with chants for Intifada and slogans that call for the end of Israel," read a recent op-ed by Victoria Rabbi Lynn Greenhough in the Victoria Times-Colonist.
In the article, Greenhough detailed how she had planned to personally stand in the way of an anti-Israel car rally planned for Dec. 31 - leading to a call from a Victoria police constable warning her that it was illegal to block a roadway.
"When I pointed out that over the past months Victoria roadways were blocked on a weekly basis, she replied, 'We can't arrest 500 people,'" wrote Greenhough.
Victoria also happens to be home of Susan Kim, a city councillor notable as one of only two Canadian politicians to sign a November petition dismissing widespread evidence that Hamas terrorists committed massrape on Oct. 7. The other signatory was Sarah Jama, an Ontario MPP who was kicked out of the Ontario NDP after justifying the massacres as an act of decolonization.
While the Plenty Collective has yielded the most brazen example of funds being distributed to anti-Israel demonstrators, similar charges have emerged in Montreal.
In December, Postmedia columnist Warren Kinsella published allegations from pro-Israel journalist Beryl Wajsman that activists had divided Montreal into a grid, with paid captains co-ordinating the demonstrations in each grid.
SURJ Toronto, a far-left "anti-racist" collective, has solicited donations for a "Community Defence Fund" to support the local chapter of the Palestinian Youth Movement - a perennial organizer of anti-Israel rallies, including a series of celebratory demonstrations held within hours of the Oct. 7 massacres in Israel.
According to Community Defence Fund's website, it has amassed $78,071.
Fundraising is helped by the fact that some of the key players in Canada's anti-Israel movement have non-profit status.
This is true of Samidoun, a Vancouver-based organization with strong links to the Popular Front for the Liberation of Palestine, a Gaza terror group believed to have participated in the Oct. 7 massacres. Both Germany and Israel list Samidoun as a terrorist entity, and research by Israel's Ministry of Strategic Affairs has claimed it is "likely" that some of the group's Canadian donations are finding their way to the PFLP.
Long before Oct. 7, Samidoun's non-profit status was the subject of a request for information by Conservative MP Melissa Lantsman, who petitioned the minister of public safety as to why it hadn't been denied certification due to its links to terror.
A reply from the ministry, issued in 2022, said that "the Government of Canada takes terrorist threats against Canada and its citizens seriously."
Non-profit status has also been extended to Palesign, one of the groups organizing a blockade of the Avenue Road Bridge in Toronto - a freeway overpass adjoining one of Canada's Jewish neighbourhoods. (On Thursday, Toronto police said protests would no longer be allowed at that location.)
Palesign has no records or web presence prior to Oct. 7.
National Post

Randall Denley: A cure for paperwork and the solution for Ontario's family doctor shortage

Author: Denley, Randall

Publication info: Denley, Randall. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/blogs-podcasts-websites/randall-denley-cure-paperwork-solution-ontarios/docview/2913488020/se-2?accountid=15090

Document 50 of 104

Ontario government's partnership with ServiceOntario and Staples Canada raises questions

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/blogs-podcasts-websites/ontario-governments-partnership-with/docview/2913479992/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Apq1busgeneral&amp;atitle=Ontario+government%27s+partnership+with+ServiceOntario+and+Staples+Canada+raises+questions&amp;title=National+Post+%28Online%29&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-11&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=National+Post+%28Online%29&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The Ontario government is reportedly closing an undisclosed number of ServiceOntario locations as it moves forward with a plan to open new centres in select Staples Canada stores.
The government announced the change in December, stating it was "making it easier and more convenient for families and businesses to access vital government services." It added that the new scheme would "reduce the overall cost to deliver government services to the public."
The announcement did not mention any closures. Citing unnamed sources within the premier's office, CityNews reported that an unspecified number of ServiceOntario centres are set to close and will have new locations open in Staples Canada stores.
There are 275 ServiceOntario locations currently operating in the province under a mix of private and provincial ownership. A 2013 Auditor General of Ontario report found that out of the then-289 ServiceOntario centres, 82 were provincially run and 207 were privately owned.
ServiceOntario employees colluded with auto theft ring again, police say
River rat with scary orange teeth added to Ontario's invasive species list
CityNews reported that some of the locations set to close have been family businesses for decades and they were given just 70 days' notice of the impending closures.
ServiceOntario says it completes 59 million transactions annually, and private operators are paid a commission for each transaction they process. It helps residents get vital documents, such as health cards, birth certificates and driver's licences.
The government source told CityNews that Staples Canada was chosen after lengthy consultations with possible retail partners, including factoring in the number of locations, the size of the stores and parking availability, among other considerations.
The source said that Staples Canada was chosen as a partner because it met all its criteria and the shift will give customers "a 30 per cent improvement to current service accessibility hours."
The reaction to the announcement on social media has been mixed, with some Ontarians, particularly in rural areas, expressing concerns about the impact the change could have on services in their communities, and the distances they would have to travel to access Staples Canada locations.
Staples Canada currently operates in 73 cities in Ontario. The privately held business was founded in 1991 and is headquartered in Richmond Hill, Ont.
Catherine Fife, the official opposition NDP critic for the finance and treasury board, said the move "raises serious concerns about Ontarians' access to government services.
"Closing down ServiceOntario locations without details of how many or how service demands will be managed, especially in Northern and rural parts of the province, is worrisome," she said "This is not an expansion of service, but another attempt for Ford to quietly hand over more of our public services to private corporations."
The financial details of the partnership, including what cost savings are expected, have not been disclosed.
